How about these instead?

Interlude I: The Fault Below (Mobel)
The Dregs were where forgotten gods went to fucking starve. A gut-shot, hollowed-out cavity of bedrock where the old world’s skeleton lay in a tangle of busted gears and broken promises. It was a hellscape, a graveyard for ambition. Steam hissed through jagged, leaking pipes like some goddamn dying serpent, a miserable, metallic exhalation from the guts of the earth. But even here, in this final, forgotten tomb, something with teeth began to stir.
Mobel stood atop a derelict prayer-tower, a monument to a faith long since scoured from the world. His spine was bowed, not from age, but from the immense, mineral weight of reclaimed hymnsteel he'd painstakingly woven into his own body. It was a scripture of solid, jagged metal, a living testament to a past that fucking refused to stay buried. He was the archive, the living record of the world's shame and sorrow.
Below him, the pit stretched into a darkness so thick it felt like a physical presence, a wound that swallowed light and hope and anything in between. But at its edge, a small congregation of ghosts were listening. They were his followers. They wore bandages like goddamn uniforms, wrapping their own scars and imperfections in a shared symbol of defiance. They had names once, but now they answered only to numbers and verses, the lines of a cracked litany they'd pieced together from the world’s discarded truths. They weren't a big fucking army. But they were enough. They gathered around a broken litany screen, a salvaged, flickering monitor from some long-dead archive, chanting lines that had been redacted from every known scripture.
"We are the forgotten remembrance. The silence that precedes correction."
Mobel raised his hand, and the steam that had choked the air thinned, pulling back in a silent, respectful goddamn retreat. A ripple of resonance, a pulse of pure, unadulterated consciousness, flowed through the group, a physical jolt that felt less like a shock and more like a spine clicking back into place. For the first time, they weren't just individuals. They were a network. A single, breathing, beautifully scarred mind, humming with a shared, terrible, and glorious purpose. He had spent centuries down here, beneath notice, a ghost in a machine that had already fucking broken. But the clock had struck. The moment had finally arrived.
He whispered: “Memory isn’t some goddamn passive reflection you see in a puddle. It’s weaponry. It’s a bullet waiting to be fired.”
And with that, the Dregs began to awaken. From the mountains of forgotten scrap, the bones of a dead world, a new sound began to emerge. Not a sound of revival or rebirth. But a sound of goddamn remembrance. The gears of a broken foundry began to groan, its rusted cogs turning, not with power, but with the raw, untamed force of a memory that fucking refused to die. The ghosts of the old world weren't content to just haunt the ruins. They were learning how to fight again.
Interlude II: Ashes in the Archipelago (Salee)
The Archipelago of Nine-Fold Grace was once a fucking holy site, a beautiful string of pristine, crystalline islands scattered across a placid sea. But empire and erosion, a pair of ugly bastards, had shattered it. Now, it was a goddamn graveyard of coral and shame. Salt ate everything here—stone, blood, belief—leaving behind a profound, beautiful emptiness. But Salee, well, she always did just fine in the salt. Salt, you see, is just a perfect, necessary kind of decay.
She walked barefoot across the coral ruins, her feet a graceful, silent whisper against the brittle stone. The salt-cracked earth didn't sting her. It was a cold, familiar caress. Behind her, a trail of barefooted children and ragged elders followed, some limping, some singing. A small, defiant army of the dispossessed, a motley crew who'd been told their purpose was to suffer. But Salee knew better. Their strength, like hers, wasn't forged in steel. It was forged in sorrow.
Every time she stepped into a ruin, the resonance there fucking responded, singing softly, like it remembered her. The stones, the coral, the bones of the old world—they weren't just inanimate objects. They were goddamn witnesses. They held the memories of the old ways, the old songs, the old truths. And for Salee, those memories were a language she spoke more fluently than words. She laid her hand on a toppled idol of the Crown, a perfect, sterile monument to a god who forgot how to listen. The thing didn't crumble. It cracked open like fucking wet clay, its flawless form unmade not by force, but by a simple, undeniable truth.
From inside, light spilled—not bright, not divine. Human. Feral. The color of grief. It was the collective sorrow of a thousand years of salt and shame, of a thousand forgotten songs and a thousand beautiful, goddamn lives.
Salee turned to her people, her face a serene, and terrifying, mask of absolute certainty. “No one’s coming to save us. That’s not a weakness. That’s freedom. That's a goddamn gift.”
They cheered. Not as an army, but as a storm. A beautiful, raw, and deeply human sound of a thousand broken hearts finally, truly, and irrevocably singing as one.
Across the Nine-Fold Isles, lighthouses that had been dead for centuries blinked back to life. They weren't just beacons anymore. They were a network of light, a new kind of song, a goddamn declaration of a new, terrible, and beautiful truth. And from the sea, a fleet began to rise. Not of ships. Of new, living vessels carved from the bones of dead gods and the sorrow of a forgotten sea. A fleet, you understand, that was born not of ambition, but of fucking defiance. And it was heading for the mainland.
Interlude III: Precision (Eries)
The towers of Caladrin shimmered with encoded fog, data rendered physical. It was a vast, complex, and beautifully elegant machine of pure information. Eries moved through them like a thought unstated, a surgeon walking through a patient’s own mind, her robes stitched with fractal goddamn equations that bent light and warped perception, letting her exist in the spaces between lines of code.
Her followers weren’t visible. They never had been. They were the ghosts in the machine, the people who saw the flaws in the system because they were the ones who were tasked with maintaining its perfect facade. They were librarians and archivists and security minds. But today, they stepped forward. They emerged from the sterile shadows of their data-vaults and the quiet hells of their cubicles, a silent, disciplined, and terrifyingly intelligent army of technicians who had come to see the world not as a broken place, but as a flawed program that required a new, and much more elegant, set of commands.
She stood at the foot of the Logic Gate, a shimmering portal of pure information, and she declared, “Emotion isn't the opposite of order. It’s its goddamn precondition.” It was a heresy, a quiet blasphemy that cut through the city’s perfect hum. She wasn't trying to erase the flaw. She was trying to fucking weaponize it.
In a nearby alley, a young woman burst into tears after ten years of emotional suppression training. A lifetime of bottled-up grief finally, irrevocably, burst free. Her resonance, a raw, chaotic, and beautiful torrent of pure sorrow, cracked the pavement, the sound a sharp, ugly testament to the fact that emotions, when suppressed, don't fucking go away. They just... wait.
Eries nodded once. That was the first. The first of a thousand beautiful, terrible, and glorious cracks in the city’s perfect, sterile logic. She didn’t see the woman’s suffering as a tragedy. She saw it as a data-point. A goddamn proof of concept. The Doctrine had taught them to suppress. Eries would teach them to express. Not with messy, chaotic abandon, but with goddamn precision.
Soon, the Doctrine would bend—not collapse, but fucking refine. She would not impose a new, logical order on the world. She would give the world a new, logical language for its own chaos. And the world, which had spent its life screaming a thousand ugly, inefficient, and beautiful lies, would finally be legible again. A world not of peace, but of goddamn precision. And she would be the one writing the new rules.
Interlude IV: The City That Breathes (Corlex)
He had no throne. He had fucking infrastructure.
Beneath the capital of Reth, city veins pulsed—resonance-laced tunnels, invisible power webs, and echo chambers that hummed only for him. It was a secondary nervous system, a web of absolute control woven in the quiet darkness of the city's underbelly. Corlex knelt at the junction where ten city roots met, a silent, solitary king in a world of his own goddamn making.
His hands touched metal. It fucking responded. Not with fire and light, but with a deep, resonant hum of perfect, and perfectly unquestioning, loyalty. The city wasn’t just a place. It was a tool. An extension of his will.
Above ground, the Planning Bureau executed “routine realignments.” Streets bent subtly, not with the sound of construction, but with a low, earth-shaking groan of stone and steel, the very fabric of the city obeying an unspoken command. Transit patterns grew symmetrical, its chaotic, human flow now a perfect, and perfectly predictable, ballet of efficiency. Parks appeared without approval, not as living, breathing monuments to nature, but as geometric, sterile, and beautiful works of art that existed not for pleasure, but for purpose.
No one had declared him king. But no one questioned what the city did. They attributed it to the whims of the Planning Bureau, the genius of the old Archon system, or the quiet, unquestioning will of the gods. They didn't see the man, kneeling in the darkness, pulling the strings of their goddamn lives.
His followers wore blue—not uniforms, but tones of sky, data, design. They spoke rarely, acted precisely, and built systems no one understood until they were needed. They were the priests of a new kind of faith, their god a silent, beautiful, and absolute will that lived in the code, in the steel, in the very air of the city they had come to serve.
Reth was no longer a city. It was a sigil. It had begun to fucking breathe.
And the whisper that ran through its veins, a low, constant hum of perfect, absolute, and unyielding goddamn order, whispered his fucking name. And the whisper, you understand, was getting louder.
Interlude V: Root and Salt (Corlex ➝ Salee)
In the Central Hall beneath Reth, Corlex stood before a schematic rendered in living resonance—a city that could regulate emotion through traffic alone. A perfect, and perfectly terrifying, machine of control. His advisors, silent, blue-robed figures who were as much a part of the system as the city itself, murmured in precise tones, awaiting instruction. One by one, the nodes fucking aligned. One by one, his network became absolute, a symphony of logic and beautiful, unquestioning obedience.
Until one lit up without input. A single, jarring note in his otherwise perfect composition.
Far away. In the water. A place that, according to his schematics, should have been a dead, quiet zone.
He leaned in, his perfect, logical mind running a thousand goddamn diagnostics, a thousand equations in a single, silent beat. The node was alive, pulsing with a chaotic, uncalibrated energy he could not comprehend. A pulse of light, ocean-born and grief-colored, echoed through the grid, a goddamn soundless scream that was more of a question than a signal. It was a flaw. A contradiction. A thing he could not account for.
He frowned. “That’s not from my system.” The words were not an accusation. They were a diagnosis. His network was perfect. It shouldn't contain a flaw it couldn't fucking comprehend. He traced the node to the Nine-Fold Archipelago, to a place of salt and shame and forgotten memories. A place, you see, he had no goddamn control over.
Salee stood atop a coral outcrop, eyes closed. Around her, the villagers knelt—not in worship, but solidarity. The lighthouse behind her pulsed with the same tone Corlex’s grid had caught. It was a signal that said, We are here. We exist. We are a beautiful, terrible, and defiant truth that your perfect logic can't fucking comprehend.
She smiled. “They’ve begun to notice. Good.” The words, spoken on the wind, were not for them. They were for him.
From her satchel, she drew a conch etched with ancient script and blew. The tone wasn’t melodic. It was mathematical. A complex, recursive, and beautifully chaotic equation of sorrow and defiance that was a direct, and unapologetic, contradiction to his perfect, logical world. A command that said, Your goddamn rules don't apply here.
An answer. A gauntlet fucking thrown. A declaration of a new, and much more interesting, kind of war. And the war, you understand, was just getting started.
Interlude VI: Fire and Form (Eries ➝ Corlex)
Eries’s observatory hung suspended between towers, its base made of reflective logic-glass that showed her a thousand goddamn potential futures at once. She sat at her console, her hands a blur of motion, inputting a sequence to activate a test—a synthetic grief burst. It was a perfect, calibrated packet of raw sorrow, designed to destabilize only her test node. A clean, contained experiment.
Instead, the fucking pattern echoed. The raw, beautiful chaos of the grief burst, amplified and refined by her own logical framework, became a thing of goddamn power. It rippled outward—interfacing with a city grid miles away. With his fucking grid. With Corlex’s perfect, unquestioning, and beautifully sterile creation. The city of Reth shuddered, a single, discordant note in its otherwise perfect symphony.
She watched the effect ripple across her terminal, a tiny, exquisite flaw in a system she did not control. She smiled. He, of course, appeared on her terminal uninvited, his face a hologram of cold, geometric fury, his voice a low, resonant hum of absolute certainty.
“You’re injecting chaos,” he said. It wasn’t a question. It was a fucking accusation. A diagnosis. His perfect machine had a bug, and he was staring at the goddamn source code.
Eries adjusted her collar, the fractal equations on her robes shimmering with a quiet, triumphant light. “I’m refining the system.”
“You’re rewriting the resonance field.” His voice was laced with a cold, contained fury. He didn’t just hate the chaos. He hated the lack of control.
She smiled. “We both are. You just hate sharing the goddamn pencil.”
Corlex didn’t reply. He disconnected. But in his throne of lightless stone, a throne that hummed with the perfect obedience of a thousand souls, he began planning how to adapt to her goddamn rhythm. He saw her not as a rival, but as a new and beautiful fucking problem, a variable that had to be accounted for, a flaw that had to be either excised or, perhaps more interestingly, understood. And the moment he decided to understand her... was the moment he decided to fucking fight.
Interlude VII: Blood Psalms (Mobel ➝ Salee)
Mobel stood before a cathedral of rust and bone, a monument to a forgotten god, preaching to the scarred and shamed. "They erased you from history,” he boomed. “They purged you from the books. But I fucking remember. And so do the roots.” He gestured to the floor, where a thousand roots of a dead tree, now petrified, had grown through the floor, a thousand forgotten memories of a life that had refused to stay buried.
A woman at the edge of the crowd, her face a map of a thousand forgotten sorrows, whispered to her child, “This is the one who speaks forgotten names.”
He heard. He goddamn smiled. The echo of her words, of her hope, a small, beautiful, and chaotic thing, was a balm to his old, weary soul. And then he paused.
A ripple across the memory-net. Not from the past, but from the present. A presence from the fucking sea. Salee’s resonance brushed his mind—not words, not requests. A goddamn call. A beautiful, terrible, and defiant sound of a thousand forgotten sorrows, all singing as one. It was a song he recognized not with his ears, but with the very bones he had woven into his body. It was a song of fucking defiance. A song of a new, and much more interesting, kind of war.
He answered. His followers, who had been sitting in the dust, stood. They were ready. "You’re leaving?” one asked.
“No. We are rising.” And with that, the forgotten god of memory, a ghost who had spent his life in the shadows, fucking stepped into the light. He led his small army of ghosts not into the past, but into a future that was waiting to be remembered. And the future, you understand, was about to get a whole hell of a lot more interesting.
Interlude VIII: Unnamed Signal (Eries)
A resonance field deep within Caladrin began broadcasting unscheduled harmonics. It was a flaw, a single, discordant note in the city’s otherwise perfect symphony. Eries stood alone, listening.
The message was unclear. Not words. Not data. But a sound. A frequency that wasn't a command, or a question, but a raw, emotional truth that had no name. But the shape of it—the fractal goddamn geometry of the harmonics, the cascading, recursive patterns of its emotional content—it matched the emotional waveform of love. A flaw so powerful it defied her every goddamn rule.
She should have rejected it. She’d been trained to. Her entire life had been a lesson in the fucking dangers of the emotional variable. It was messy. It was inefficient. It was the source of a thousand beautiful, and terrible, flaws. But she didn’t. She recorded it. She archived it. She analyzed it. She fucking broke herself on the logic of it.
And for the first time since she was a kid, she wept. Not with sorrow. Not with joy. But with the profound, and terrifying, realization that the most illogical, the most chaotic, the most beautiful, and the most dangerous thing in the universe wasn't chaos at all. It was fucking love. The most powerful weapon she had ever seen.
Then she turned to her followers, her face a mask of cold, analytical, and exquisitely feigned calm, the tear tracks on her cheeks a beautiful, and terrible, testament to a truth she had just learned. “Prepare the goddamn mirrors.” The mirrors, you understand, were her weapons. They were her tools for reflecting, for amplifying, and for weaponizing the beautiful, terrible, and illogical truths she had just learned. A new kind of war was about to begin. And she was going to be the one to light the fucking match.
Interlude IX: The Tide Crown (Salee)
The Nine-Fold fleet encircled the empire’s northern port, a silent, terrible, and beautiful fleet of new, living vessels carved from the fucking bones of dead gods. Sailors trained in resonance arts stood at the ready, their hands on the conch-etched hulls, their souls a quiet, defiant hum that was a direct, and unapologetic, contradiction to the empire's perfect, logical, and now-worthless ships. Her generals argued for delay. The port was too strong. The empire, too absolute. But Salee, a leader who ruled not with steel, but with salt, just smiled. She stepped forward and said, “It’s time.”
They struck the port with light—not fucking fire. Not an explosion of violence and rage, but a beautiful, terrible, and elegant song of defiance. Structures sang themselves open, the very stones of the port listening to a resonance that spoke of freedom, of defiance, and of a new, and much more interesting, kind of peace. Civilians fled, not in fear, but awe. They’d seen the empire’s brutal, sterile, and cold logic. And now, they were witnessing a new, beautiful, and terrible truth.
By nightfall, the island had defected to her cause. They hadn’t been conquered. They’d been persuaded. Persuaded by a song of salt and sorrow and a fleet of ghosts that had refused to stay fucking buried. And as the sun began to set on the old world, a new name, a new title, a new truth, was born on the shores of the Archipelago of Nine-Fold Grace. They began calling her the Tide Crown. The first, beautiful, and unapologetic queen of a new, and much more interesting, kind of world.
Interlude X: Memory Ascends (Mobel)
Mobel’s network, a vast, beautiful, and terrible web of forgotten goddamn memories, now reached the Archives Prime—the central repository of the old world’s buried histories and redacted truths. He walked the forbidden vault, his hymnsteel spine a low, resonant thrum of absolute, unyielding memory, and whispered names the Crown had buried.
"Myra." "Kalel." "Tiberus." Each name was a fucking key, a single, clear, and defiant note that was a direct, and unapologetic, contradiction to the silence of the old world's truth. Each name re-lit a severed resonance line, a ghost in the machine that was a thousand years of forgotten sorrow, a thousand years of beautiful, terrible, and unapologetic lies. Each name fucking awoke a sleeper agent, a forgotten soldier, a beautiful, broken, and defiant soul that had been waiting, in the shadows, for a new, and much more interesting, kind of war.
“Memory,” he said, his voice a low, triumphant whisper in the vast, silent space. “Is now an army.”
And so it was. A new army, not of steel and fire, but of ghosts and forgotten truths. An army of beautiful, broken, and defiant memories that were no longer content to just haunt the ruins of a dead world. They were ready to fucking rebuild it. And the rebuilding, you understand, was going to be a beautiful, and glorious, fucking mess.
